## Page 4

Dear Ravi,

I am a person who has been living without knowing what tears are for the past ten years, but after watching the serial Heli Hogu Karana, I was moved by the character of Prarthana. For some reason, I felt a lump in my throat and I cried. I am a person who leads a life like a candy seller. That's why the girl is very far from me. I request you to please complete the novel and put an end to my curiosity, Vaishesh Lakshmeeshwar. I am writing this letter to you, and you are a reader of mine whom I have not met yet, Mahesh, Ravi Belagere.

---

## Page 5

I have been wandering around the country, and my weight, which was once around one hundred and six kilograms, has now reduced to sixty-nine kilograms. It all started with the interview of Kola Fayaz, the murderer from Shivajinagar, and my journey in journalism, which has taken me to the depths of Italy, has been a long one. I have come to realize that writing without investigation, research, and emotion is not worth it, and it years to understand this. I have traveled to more than thirty countries and have written about various topics such as love, history, lust, war, terrorism, cinema, and more. I have been fortunate enough to have been given the Aktha award, and I am content.

I have also ventured into television, and I am the executive officer of Janashree TV. It took me twenty-four years to receive my first Sahitya Akademi award, and received the Shivaram Karanth Award, the Masti Award, and the Rajyotsava Award. I have translated the autobiography of a Telugu writer, and I have received the Kuvempu Bhasha Bharati Award for it. The biggest award is from the auto drivers of Bangalore, who have given me the "Sakkathaga Bariythaane" award.

I have also acted in a few Kannada films, and I have written several books, including "Haay Bangalore" and "Omanase". I have a book store in Gandhi Bazaar, Bangalore, called "BBC" (Belagere Book Center and Coffee). mix of fans and critics, two daughters and two sons, as well as two wives and four grandchildren. My weaknesses include smoking, traveling, reading, writing, music, and history. The world is my home, and I am always wondering what to do next.

## Page 6

They walked on, both of them, in the shade, their feet moving in unison, as if they were on a well-practiced routine. He had his hands stuffed into the pockets of his pants, and she had her arms wrapped tightly around her chest. Their conversation had reached a point where they were both silent, lost in their own thoughts. The chill of the Shivamogga evening was biting, and they had already eaten half of the rice that had been served for dinner.

"Why are you so far away?" he asked, his voice low and husky. "If we cross the Shivamurthy Circle, it's only a five-minute walk." "You can see the circle from here," she replied, her voice barely above a whisper. "If you start talking to me, will you not shiver with cold?" "And get hungry?" he asked, his eyes scanning her face. "What are you afraid of?" she asked, her voice firm, but her eyes betraying a hint of uncertainty.

Her name was Prarthana, and this was not her first walk. She had walked from Channarayapatna to Arasikere, and he had been with her every step of the way, his hand in hers, his fingers intertwined with hers. "If we walk a little further," he said, "we'll reach the Kadur road. If we turn left there, we'll reach the Beeroor village." "And right," she said, her voice barely above a whisper, forest. If we walk a little further, tree's shadow, and the evening breeze will be cool." "But too far," he said, his voice low and husky, "we'll reach the Tarikere village. And far from there, it will take hours to reach Shivamogga."

He was lost in thought, his eyes scanning the horizon, his mind racing with the possibilities. She was quiet, her eyes fixed on the ground, her feet moving in unison with his. They had been walking for hours, and the sun was setting, casting a golden glow over the landscape. "When we reach Shivamogga," he said, his voice low and husky, "we'll be safe. We'll be close to home." "But for now," she said, her voice barely above a whisper, "let's just walk."

## Page 7

If she had not walked with him, she would have got lost somewhere along the way, she thought. Her ankle, which she had twisted near Tarikere, had started to pain and must be bleeding. She dragged her feet with effort. Himavant looked at her affectionately and tightened his grip in his pocket. The cold was beginning to numb their fingers. "Who are you?" a hoarse voice called out, making them both startle. The voice came unexpectedly from the darkness in the northeastern corner of Shivamurthy Circle. Behind it emerged a night-patrol police constable wearing a heavy coat. His heavy boots clicked on the ground. Under the street light, his stubbled face looked even harsher. "Who are you?" he asked again, coming closer. A bidi burned angrily between his fingers. For some reason, he suddenly reminded her of her father! "I am Himavant, her name is Prarthana," Himavant answered in a calm voice. "Where are you coming from?" the constable asked. "From Channarayapatna." "At what time? Which bus? Where is the ticket?" The questions came firing from the constable's mouth. "We didn't come by bus, we walked. Where would we get a ticket? We need to go home," Himavant replied fluently. The constable's stubbled face was filled with surprise. Walking from Channarayapatna? Are they crazy? "You should at least lie convincingly. Come to the station. You seem to have brought a girl along, who knows if there's a missing case registered. The inspector isn't nice. You know how sub-inspectors are? Very strict." "I will tell you once more. My name is Himavant, she is Prarthana. We are both over eighteen. If you call, we will come anywhere in the morning. Past the circle, it's a five-minute walk. I live in the backyard of merchant Shantayya's house. We didn't walk because we didn't have bus fare. I have one hundred and twenty rupees in my pocket." Himavant was about to say something else. "Then why did you walk?" the constable interrupted. "We want to walk together for the rest of our lives, at least till Shivamogga, to see if we could do it through grit. There are only five kilometers left...

## Page 9

Himavant stood at the door, watching Prarthana tend to her wound with a bandage. "Did you walk ten kilometers?" he asked. "Does our love have the strength to overcome anything?" He then asked, "Did you take nine steps before the seven verses?" Prarthana replied, "Who counted?" Himavant asked, "How far is it from Channarayapatna to Shivamogga?" Prarthana said, "I don't know."

Meanwhile, Himavant's thoughts drifted to his family. His brother was probably milking the cow, and his mother was probably crying. He didn't think about searching for them. Prarthana was lost in thought, standing at the door. Himavant opened the window and let in fresh air. He changed his clothes, put on a lungi, and went to the kitchen to make tea. He lit the stove and put water to boil. Prarthana, still standing at the door, suddenly felt a chill run down her spine. "Come in," she said. "We have to start a new life from now on."

Himavant poured water from the tap into a pot. "Which gods are we praying to?" he asked. Prarthana replied, "I don't know." Himavant put his left foot into the water. "Prarthana, you first," he said. Prarthana hesitated for a moment before stepping Her right foot hurt, and she winced in pain. Himavant noticed and suggested that she put in first. But Prarthana's right foot was the one that had been injured, and she had put it in first.

Himavant's left foot was in the water, and he was trying to make it comfortable for Prarthana. But the water was too hot, getting burned. He took out his foot and put it back in, but it still hurt. He tried but it was no use. again and let it cool down. Prarthana was still standing at the door, watching him.

After a while, Himavant got out of the water and dried himself. He looked at Prarthana and asked, "Are you okay?" Prarthana nodded. Himavant then went to the door and looked out. He saw Rasool Jamadar, who was sitting on a bench, staring at him. Rasool's old bicycle was parked nearby.

Thus, starting from Channarayapatna, the two unclear shadows walked and entered the small room in the backyard of merchant Shantappa’s house near Shivamurthy Circle that night, unnoticed by anyone, except for Rasool Jamadar. He had quietly walked behind them, stood at the door to confirm that both had reached their room, and then returned.
For those shadows, there was no other shelter in the whole world. One shadow was struggling in helplessness, while the other held its hand in consent.

Prarthana's eyes were filled with tears; she had escaped from her own hell. She had been running for sixteen days, seventeen nights, in the bus from Channarayapatna. She didn't have a single rupee in her pocket. Her dreams. "What do I want to become, Prarthana? a doctor, Himavant! be a mother who can give birth to lakhs of children, who can be like Amma. A flower from the deity's right arm in the temple was slowly falling down, and it was as if it was saying, "Prarthana will become a doctor!" ## Page 13

Himavant sat up, trying to appear as if he had not been paying attention. Prarthana's eyes were closed, was trying to remember something. It had been a while since he had spoken, and now recall what he had said. He had been sitting there, staring at Prarthana, but it was all a blur.

He was a man who lived by routine, waking up early in the morning to do his chores, and finishing them by evening. He had a habit of remembering everything he had to do, and doing it on time. But today, something was different. Today, he was feeling restless, and his mind was wandering.

As he sat there, he thought about his son, Narasimayya, who was a student at the local school. He was a good boy, and Himavant was proud of him. He remembered the day Narasimayya had started going to school, and how he had been so excited to see him off. He had even made a point to stand outside the school, so that everyone in the village could see him, and know that he was a respectable man.

But now, as he sat there, he couldn't help but think about the rumors that had been circulating about Narasimayya. People were saying that he was not a good student, and living up to his potential. Himavant tried to push these thoughts away, but they kept coming back to him.

Just then, he heard the sound of a motorbike outside. It was Shivashankarayya, the school inspector, who had come to inspect the school. Himavant's heart sank, as he thought about the rumors that might be spread about Narasimayya. He quickly got up, and went to the door, trying to compose himself.

As he opened the door, he saw Shivashankarayya standing there, looking at him with a mixture of curiosity and annoyance. "What's going on here?" he asked, his voice firm but not unkind. Himavant tried to explain, but Shivashankarayya cut him off. "I'm here to inspect the school, not to talk to you," he said, his tone firm.

Himavant nodded, trying to hide his embarrassment. He knew that he had been caught off guard, and not been prepared for the inspector's visit. He quickly stepped aside, allowing Shivashankarayya to enter the house.

As the inspector walked in, Himavant couldn't help but notice the look of disdain on his face. It was as if he was looking at something dirty, something that was beneath him. Himavant felt a surge of anger, but he tried to keep it in check. He knew that he had to be careful, respectful to the inspector, no matter how he felt.

Shivashankarayya walked into the house, his eyes scanning the room with a mixture of curiosity and disdain. Himavant followed him, trying to keep up with his pace. As they walked, Himavant couldn't help but think about the rumors that had been circulating about Narasimayya. He knew that he had to do something to clear his son's name, but he didn't know what.

As they reached the school, Himavant saw Narasimayya standing there, looking nervous. He was a good boy, and Himavant was proud of him. But as he looked at him, he saw something in his eyes that gave him pause. It was a look of fear, uncertainty. Himavant's heart sank, as he realized that his son was in trouble.

## Page 15

Himavant was making a story out of nothing. In the dead of night, Prarthana entered the room, her face lit up by the moonlight. The room was as quiet as a tomb, but she noticed a faint noise coming from the corner of the room. She saw a figure huddled under a blanket, its head resting on a bundle of clothes. It was Himavant, his arms and shoulders wrapped in a thick cloth, like a woman carrying a load of firewood on her head. His massive were as sturdy as a rock. Prarthana wondered how he could even move with such a heavy load on his shoulders.

As she approached him, Himavant looked up and smiled, his eyes twinkling with mischief. "Banni, banni, banni," he said, his voice low and husky. Prarthana's heart skipped a beat as she saw the old man, Shantappa, enter the room, his eyes fixed on the bundle of clothes. He was a stout man, with a round belly and a wispy beard. He wore a dhoti and a vest, and his hair was greying at the temples. On his chest, silver pendant, shaped like a linga, and a garland of flowers around his neck. He looked like a Brahmin priest, but his eyes were shrewd and cunning.

Shantappa's eyes scanned the room, and his gaze fell on the bundle of clothes. He walked towards it, his eyes fixed on it, and his hands reached out to touch it. "Kootakali," he said, his voice low and menacing. "What kind of person are you, to be carrying such a heavy load?" He looked at Himavant, his eyes narrowing. "Are you a fool, or a thief?" Himavant's smile faltered, and he looked down, his eyes avoiding Shantappa's gaze.

Shantappa's voice was like a whip, cracking with anger. "You are a fool, to be carrying such a heavy load. You are a thief, to be hiding it in my house." Himavant's eyes flashed with anger, but he said nothing. Shantappa's eyes narrowed, and he looked at Prarthana. "And you, girl, what are you doing here?" Prarthana's heart was racing, and she stuttered out an answer. "I-I came to study, sir. I'm a student." Shantappa's eyes scanned her face, and he nodded. "Very well, you may stay. But you must be careful, my house is not a place for idle chatter."

Himavant's eyes met Prarthana's, and he smiled, his eyes twinkling with mischief. Shantappa's eyes followed his gaze, and his face darkened. "What is this, Himavant? You are a fool, to be making friends with this girl. She is a stranger, and you don't know her." Himavant's smile faltered, and he looked down, his eyes avoiding Shantappa's gaze. Shantappa's voice was like a whip, cracking with anger. "You are a fool, Himavant, to be trusting this girl. She may be a thief, or a spy."

## Page 17

Let Himavant's decisions be. He was the kind of person who would make decisions without consulting anyone else, even if it meant going against the norms. That's why Prarthana had asked him, "Doctor, what's the point of asking if you're going to do what you want anyway?" But Himavant's decisions were not something that could be changed easily. So, he had decided to go to Davaanagere, and Prarthana had agreed to go with him.

As they walked, Prarthana couldn't help but think about how far away Davaanagere was. If she had to walk, it would take her a whole day to get there. Even if she took a bus, at least an hour. And what some more? She had seen the bus stop on the way, but she hadn't seen any other buses. She had no idea how long it would take to get to Davaanagere.

As they walked, Prarthana couldn't help but think about how different their town was from the other towns and villages they had seen on the way. small and peaceful, but the other places were bustling with activity. She had seen people walking in the streets, and shops and houses lined up one after the other. It was a completely different world from the one she was used to.

As they walked, Prarthana couldn't help but think about how much she missed her home. She had never been to Davaanagere before, and she wasn't sure what to expect. But she knew that she had to go with Himavant, no matter what. She was his wife, after all, and support him in everything he did.

As they walked, Prarthana couldn't help but think about how much she loved Himavant. He was a good man, and he always put her needs before his own. She knew that he was going to Davaanagere for a reason, and she was determined to find out what that reason was.

As they walked, Prarthana saw a small village in the distance. It was surrounded by fields and trees, and there was a small stream running through it. She had never seen a village like that before, and she was curious to know more about it. She asked Himavant to take her to the village, and he agreed.

As they walked into the village, Prarthana saw a group of people sitting under a tree. They were eating and drinking, and they looked happy and content. She saw a small shop in the center of the village, and she asked Himavant to take her there. He agreed, and they walked towards the shop.

As they walked, Prarthana saw a small boy sitting on the side of the road. He was eating a piece of bread, and he looked up at her with a smile. She smiled back at him, and he waved at her. She waved back, and they continued walking.

As they walked up the hill, Prarthana saw a small temple at the top. It was surrounded by trees and bushes, and there was a small stream running through it. She had never seen a temple like that before, and she was curious to know more about it. She asked Himavant to take her to the temple, and he agreed.

As they walked into the temple, Prarthana saw a group of people sitting on the floor. They were praying and chanting, and they looked peaceful and content. She saw a small idol of a god in the center of the temple, and she asked Himavant to tell her more about it. He agreed, and they sat down to listen.

As they sat there, Prarthana felt a sense of peace and contentment wash over her. She knew that she was exactly where she was meant to be, and she was grateful to Himavant for bringing her there. would always cherish this moment, have Himavant by her side.

## Page 18

It was a strange sight to see Himavant sitting under a tree, drinking water from a pot, without anyone bothering him. If you looked at him, you would think he was the closest person to Channarayapatna. His father, Aliandasandra, was like that. Aliandasandra's son, Orannaappa, was a man with a big name, born to a man who was known for his bravery. At that time, he was a young man, and he used to carry a panchanga and other things in his hand; he didn't know the days of the week, the stars, or the planets, but he was feared by people. They used to say that if he went to court and filed a case, he would win it, but if he faced someone who was stronger than him, he would run away.

But Himavant knew that his father was a man who had killed his mother and had hit his head with a big stone. They used to say that he would die by the third year, and that's why his stepmother, Appanna's sister, had left Aliandasandra's house and gone to Channarayapatna, crossing the Baragur river and settling in a house in the Kattegaratti village. It was said that Himavant was under the care of his stepmother, and that she had brought him up.

As for the question of who was born first, the mother or the child, it was a common saying that if the mother died, the child would also die. But Himavant's mother had died, and his stepmother had taken care of him. If she had also died, Prarthana would not have been born. The two of them would have been together, and their lives happy.

But Aliandasandra's daughter, Prarthana, was not born to him, and she was a girl who was loved by her father, and he used to say that she was the only one who understood him. could see the pain he was going through, and who could understand what he was feeling.

As for the question of what would happen to Prarthana, it was said that she would be married to a man who was worthy of her, and live a happy life. But Aliandasandra's son, Orannaappa, was not her. He was feared by people, and he was not loved by anyone.

But Himavant knew that his stepmother had taken care of him, and that she had brought him up. He knew that she was the one who had given him life, and had loved him. And he would never forget her, and that he would always be grateful to her.

## Page 19

'Chandranath Bandopadhyay, Number 43, Banjara Hills, Hyderabad' — she could have easily memorized those four lines without writing them down, but she felt she might forget the address after reading the letter, so she hurriedly noted it on a small slip. That night, in her rush, as soon as Urmila fell asleep in the hostel, Prarthana sat up and switched on the table lamp to write two letters that would change the course of her life. The first was written to Chandranath Bandopadhyay; the second—to her father, who had long seemed erased from the canvas of her memory. To Himavant, the boy she loved more than life itself, she never once felt the urge to write such a letter. She stood just a few steps away from the throne of her aspirations.

"Respectful greetings to Father. You may not be aware that I am studying MBBS at Davangere Medical College. Since I left home, the direction of my life has changed. Until today, I had never felt the need to share the details of all that with you. However, I have decided to marry my senior colleague, Debashish Bandopadhyay. His father is coming to speak with me from Hyderabad this Sunday. In this situation, if you were with me, it would be a great help; you, the father who gave me life. I conclude this letter with the belief that you will come. Prarthana."

She finished the second letter, noting the address. Having already sealed the first letter with the hostel's return address after writing the Hyderabad address on the envelope, the lines she had read at Debashish’s house began to echo in her mind. It was a letter written by a father to his only beloved son: "Dear Sunny; you are the center point that gives our life both joy and anxiety. When we think of you, your speech, your appearance, the marks you earn, the speed with which you win—it makes us feel there are no parents as fortunate as we are. However, the rumors and details that reach us about you leave us deeply unsettled."

---

## Page 193

Who is this girl named Prarthana? Where is she from? You probably haven't asked what her caste is, and we do not ask for 'details' about money or wealth. need that, but what kind of a girl is she? Like other friendships that have been drifting along for a long time, this one does not feel temporary. It seems as though Debu is going to marry this girl, as Uncle Shivarajayya has written in his letter. If all of this is true, there is no one happier than us. But Debu, we are worried about what kind of person your 'girl' is. Apart from you, we have absolutely no one else in this world. Because we know your nature, we are asking this: write to us and share your opinion about Prarthana. If there is a shortage of time, or if you have no objection to talking, tell us to speak over the telephone. Or, if you want me to come to Davanagere myself, I will come immediately. Debu, you are not a child. You are a grown man. Grown children become like friends. As a friend, I am asking you…

By the time Debu finished his bath and returned, Prarthana had read the letter from 'Dad' so intensely that every letter was etched in her memory. From the moment she read it, her heart began to overflow with a new enthusiasm, a new hope, and a new joy. For a girl from a poor family in a tiny village called Bilisaale, near Channarayapatna, to become the daughter-in-law of Chandranath Bidish Bandyopadhyay, the ruler of India’s pharmaceutical empire—was that just a small thing?

Prarthana’s respects to the elders. Please permit me to write this letter. I, Prarthana, am poor by caste. I am not as intelligent as Debu. But I am decent enough to be liked by you. I love Debu as much as I trust God. I have respect, regard, and love for elders. I am a girl who has grown up in the lap of struggles. Debu has loved me, and because of that, our hearts are this close even today. Restraint governs us. I do not even know if Debu has the intention to marry me yet, but I have already decided to spend my life with him. You must bless us. Debu is not one for words. He is too lazy to write a letter. I am writing this on Saturday with his permission. Please...

---

## Page 194

Come to Davangere. Decide the direction of our lives together and bring mother along; what else should I call her but 'mother', just like your love? My love waits for you and Debu until the very end. With these prayers, Prarthana thought over the letter a dozen times, placed a photograph of herself inside, and sealed the envelope. Just then, for some reason, she was reminded of Himavant. It felt as though Urmi Didi, sleeping on the cot in front of her, had turned over in impatience. Is this a sin? betrayal of God? Prarthana asked herself; she did not have the patience to seek an answer. She could not fall asleep after tucking the letters into their envelopes. Everything that had happened at Debu's house kept playing over and over in her mind. Truth be told, similar incidents had occurred in that house even before; while they seemed sweet at first, they eventually became tiresome. She often felt that if only she could escape Debu’s embrace, it would be enough. But what happened yesterday was not that. It was the complete opposite! Having just come out of the bathroom wearing only a lungi, Debu was glistening like a statue; his eyes were swollen and turned a fiery red from the night's drinking. He seemed to have lost his usual charm. However, the letter from his father that he had just read, and the mention of her within its lines, had brought such happiness to Prarthana that she had fallen into his arms as he stood there smelling fresh from his bath. If given even the slightest chance, Debu would hunger and yearn; he would fill the car with the resonance of desire in the desolate darkness of the Harihara road. More than love, a sense of urgency roamed within him; he would plead with his eyes alone, his fingers would begin to strum like a Rudraveena. She loved all of that too; such is the sweetness of romance before marriage. But it is beautiful only if it stops there, isn't it? It wouldn't stop. Whenever they were left alone at home, Debu would become like a demon. Many times, arguments would erupt precisely because of this. All this beauty, the warmth of the body, the grace; all its darkness is yours, my lord, but let there be restraint until the hour of our marriage, Prarthana would say. Debu would flare up and become annoyed. Many times, she had left him in that state and walked away. But today, she herself had been prepared. If the scandal involving the Mangalore girl had not occurred the previous day, the bud would have blossomed by the break of dawn.

---

## Page 195

She leaned into his embrace, guiding him gently toward the cot. The warmth of her chest pressing against him was an experience that felt both heavy and exhilarating for Prarthana. Debu felt comforted, and his breathing grew shallow and rapid. The moment of culmination was near. While Prarthana trembled with a surge of excitement, Debu turned rigid. He was staring fixedly at the brown bruise that had suddenly appeared on her full arm. He kept asking himself: "Tell me the truth... what happened there?" Prarthana was in tears.

"Urmi Didi, please look after me with love. Bless me so that everything goes well for me when you send me off. Tomorrow, my life will find its direction. Debu’s parents are coming; they are coming specifically to see me. The day after, my own father is also coming from Channarayapatna. Please don't be angry, Didi. I know you don't approve of this decision of mine, but what can I do? I cannot bear to be separated from Debu. I don't know why, but my inner self keeps telling me that everything will turn out well. Forgive me this one time, Urmila! Who else do I have in this world but you? Himu knows nothing of all this. I am anxious about how he will react once he finds out. He isn't exactly emotional, and that is my fear. Emotional people cry, laugh, curse, get angry, and eventually quiet down. But Urmila Didi, Himu is very introverted. I keep worrying about what he might do to himself. I get scared deep inside, but then I console myself and move on. What if I walk a different path? He will suffer for four days, and then his dreams will sprout elsewhere; he will find someone else. Himu is better than me—he is a man like a god. God will not be sad. angry.

"Is it true, Urmila Didi? You might be thinking, 'Is this the extent of your devotion?' wondering how much you truly love Debu. Before we even got intimate, Debu had become so dear to me that I felt like I would die without him. I deeply attached to him. There was a rare kind of friendship between the two of you; even if you felt angry at his generosity, you always forgave a good friend..."

---

## Page 196

"Urmila akka, before that, you must forgive me. I know I am the biggest sinner of all. But youngest, younger than you, younger than Debu, younger than God himself. Can’t you forgive me? I will be indebted to you for the rest of my life. There is no one else who can drape a saree as neatly on me as you do. Tomorrow, the groom’s family is coming to see me; if you were here, mother wouldn’t have to struggle alone, would she? In the morning, bring me a flower or a kiss, and send me home. Once I return, I will drown you in kisses, Debu."

Prarthana wrote this letter in her lovely handwriting, placed it in an envelope, and without sealing it, left it prominently on Urmila’s bed pillow. Then, as if remembering something, she wrote 'Prarthane' on the envelope. Having just seen Urmila reading very seriously in the library, she concluded that it might be quite late before she returned; so, Prarthana turned off all the lights in the room and lay down.

Sleep did not come immediately. Why did he ask that question, when he should have been responding to the invitation of pleasure? Why did the question of what Sujay might have done on the Harihar road haunt Debu, especially in such intense, final moments? Did Debu babu suddenly doubt her? Does he really think that something improper happened between her and Sujay? Impossible. Debu is not that kind of man. A man with such an open mind, who would sit for hours and talk about his hundreds of trivial details, could never harbor such petty suspicions. Perhaps he would never ask such a question again. If he were to start asking, couldn't she ask as well? Kaveramma's house; the Muslim girlfriend from his third year; that girl Hemalatha from the senior batch; and finally, Urmila didi! Did he have just one or two? Such things are known to all; in a man's life, there may be hundreds of such instances. A wife must ignore all of them. How you were until yesterday, or why you were that way—I don't need to know. From today, you are mine, only mine. And as for me, you know that I was with Himavant for months; if one starts digging, there is a history to that as well. But what is the use of digging? They are things that died long ago; digging them up only makes the stench unbearable. Forget all that. From tomorrow, a new life must branch out, Mitra. She decided to explain this to him. Her mind turned to Debu's parents.

---

## Page 197

She was reveling in the feeling of being cherished. Would she be liked by both of them? And if, by chance, they did not like her? Would she defy them and still marry Debu? But how could she know until they met? Wasn't father coming from Channarayapatna only after the first round of talks? That man would agree to this marriage; there was no question of him not agreeing. She no longer held any respect for him, yet his presence was necessary for the talks—at least for the sake of formality. If only Himavant were here at such a celebratory moment! Before she could finish the thought, Prarthana fell asleep. However, the one who tossed and turned all night without a wink of sleep was Urmila. Suddenly sitting bolt upright in the middle of the night, she felt like heading straight to Shivamogga. Realizing this was no time to leave, she recited a prayer in her mind and calmed down. Her heart would sometimes soften towards Prarthana. As she herself had said, she was the youngest of them all; let things happen as she wished. Who knew? Perhaps Debu would change. A person who has reached the extreme of decadence can suddenly discard all that indulgence and become a gentleman. Why talk of others? Look at me, for instance. Today, the very thought of a cigarette feels repulsive; the mere mention of whiskey is sickening. Debu, too, might change. Both their lives could become beautiful. But what of Himavant’s fate?

An indistinct image of Himavant began to drift across the canvas of Urmila’s mind—Himavant, sitting cross-legged in the middle of the open fields of Gadikoppa, sketching a lovely blueprint of a hospital. In his imagination, he wondered: at which portico’s shade would Prarthana’s car be parked if she arrived suddenly? And through which door could he enter to reach the pregnant woman crying out in the throes of labor most quickly? At which corner of the hospital terrace could she curl up and sing to her heart’s content when she was overjoyed? Where would the visitors meet and talk to her instead of the patients? So many dreams he built. What if she is young? Can she betray such a Himavant? Even if she does and gets away with it, will she ever be happy? One may not believe in curses, but the sigh of a love betrayed will never let anyone live in peace. Whatever else may be, once a girl comes of age, a shadow or two from an unexpected stranger is bound to drift through her life. Does she need an example better than herself? After returning from Kodagu, she had felt that life without him was no life at all. And then… he had drifted away, and Debu had arrived.

---

## Page 198

It was impossible to tell when he had come or left. A girl's heart can shatter like a fresh slate at any moment. Prarthana too would forget Debu within a couple of days. Is it even possible to live a happy life after marrying such a man? Or will life even be possible at all? But under no circumstances should Himavant be betrayed. "I will make sure no betrayal happens," she said firmly to herself and sat up. Somewhere in the distance, the first bell of the fourth watch of the night was heard. Rising hurriedly, she read Prarthana's letter one more time. She switched on the geyser. Reciting the morning shlokas in a soft voice, she gently shook Prarthana, who was in a deep slumber, and woke her up. Pressing a gentle kiss on her forehead, she whispered "Good morning" in a loving voice. Then, pouring two drops of oil from a small bottle onto her palm, she massaged it into Prarthana’s hair. For the first time in many years, it felt to Prarthana as if she were sitting bowed before her mother, getting her hair oiled and washed in the bathroom. "I will be born as your daughter in my next life, and I will be even naughtier than I am now," she said. As Prarthana stepped out from under the shower after her bath, Urmila noticed something. "What is this, Prarthana? What are these brown bruises? Has it already gone this far? You had it with Debu…?" Though Urmila felt her voice becoming harder than necessary, she couldn't control herself and asked anyway. There was an unexpected intensity in her eyes; the very thought that Prarthana had already lost everything she called her own to Debu had left Urmila shaken. But as she stared at the faded brown bruises on her arm, the tone in which she had asked the question began to feel wrong even to her. Just as she was about to ask again, Prarthana reacted in a strange manner that was far beyond Urmila’s imagination. "Urmila Didi, that is entirely my personal business. Mind you, it is true that I respect you a lot—not just today, but always. I, too, desire that every morning of my life should be graced by your good wishes. Never ask such a question ever again. And yet, since you have asked, and since you asked it so bluntly, I am telling you—even if it is a matter of self-respect, today you have..."

## Page 199

Listen, these are not marks made by Debu, it was somebody else. His name might even be Sujay Malhotra. I think this explanation is enough for you. Give me my bag," she said, and without even waiting for Urmila's reaction, Prarthana walked out of the bathroom. Urmila had already decided: she would not let Himavant be betrayed! That morning, Urmila had begun the first and greatest battle of her life.

"What am I supposed to do? You have decided that you won't leave Shivamogga for months; you have made up your mind not to even look towards Davanagere. For how long go without seeing you? I missed you so much in the early hours of the morning that I just got on a bus and came here," Urmila said, standing in front of his stone-walled room in Gaadikoppa, observing the surprise in Himavant's eyes. Even though there was fatigue in the eyes of the girl who had traveled from Davanagere, the joy of seeing Himavant was flowing in them like a flood. As the thought flashed through his mind—*has this girl started to love me?*—Himavant felt shocked for a moment. It should never be like that. Just as it feels unbearable to love someone who doesn't like us, it becomes equally difficult to endure it when someone we don't like loves us. Is it possible to share love with another soul in this life besides Prarthana? In my world, there are only two images: Prarthana and Doctor Prarthana. Even a third shadow is forbidden there. In Urmila's eyes, there might be excitement and joy, or perhaps it is just friendship. Why am I seeing it as something else? Should I misunderstand her innocent excitement? Even if Urmila feels this way, I should tell her the truth this time, he thought. But Urmila was lost in a different kind of excitement. The open field of Gaadikoppa, which she had seen months ago, had changed beyond recognition; foundations had sprouted even in the wild bushes. Everything in the middle of the field had changed. Two tall granite stone pillars had been buried; between them, a small arch had been constructed. A brass bell had been hung at a height that was within reach; mango leaves had been tied to both pillars.

---

## Page 200

"Tell me, what is the reason?" Himavant crossed the pillars. The foundation had been laid. The second floor was just rising. Like a Ganesh idol that had just been worshipped, there were signs of a temple standing on this high mound. Prarthana stood there, stunned. "What is this? It has stopped. Shouldn't there be a hospital here?" she asked, surprised. "According to the plan, a temple should be built at the foundation of every hospital. Whether God exists in such hospitals is something I don't know. But where a human exists, humanity resides. Prarthana Hospital did not rise here, but a sanctum sanctorum for a hospital is what stands on this mound. Someone told me that in the foothills of the Himalayas, they make Ganesh idols like this. I have decided to build a hospital for that Ganesh, to serve humanity within it," Himavant said, stopping the car. "First, let's go to the portico in the middle of the hospital to see the patients, and then we shall offer our salutations to Ganesh."

"Such eccentricities are always there; forget that, Dr. Prarthana. Whatever I do, it with great dedication for the sake of Prarthana. Let all that be; how is Prarthana? Is she studying well? Is she keeping well? Does she still get those stomach aches?" Himavant kept asking one question after another with unmatched affection. Urmila could not continue her speech; she began to sob. "You are very good, Himu... why do you come looking for me like this? If there is anything good, it must be in your mind or your personality. the personality of the girl named Prarthana. If not, why would we talk about her more than ourselves? The number of people in this world who are as pure as nectar is very small, Urmila. Come, shall I give you a glass of milk?" Saying this, Himavant led her towards his stone house.

Inside, more surprises awaited Urmila. From the outside, it looked like it always did, but the stone house was adorned and expanded from within, waiting for his light. Large windows with big wooden frames; deep black doors that looked as if they hadn't been carved for decades; preparations for laying carpets on the floor; and a separate inner room.

---

## Page 201

As soon as Prarthana came home from her hospital, she closed the door behind her. There was a small, neat sink; a glistening soap beside a tap to wash hands; a tiny artificial waterfall in the room; and two musical instruments lying there, as if waiting to be played. Then, another bed nearby. The beds were joined together. "Whose is the third bed? For a baby?" Urmila asked with a laugh. "No, that’s also for Prarthana. Sometimes when she comes back after a night shift, she feels like just collapsing and sleeping, doesn't she? So, I made this arrangement so I wouldn't disturb her when I come in!" Himavant said. For the second time that day, Urmila’s eyes brimmed with tears. Attached to the bedroom was a room specifically designed for Prarthana; shelves were built from the floor all the way to the ceiling just to store her books. The lighting was arranged so that her eyes wouldn't strain no matter where she sat to read. There was an easy chair to lean back on if she grew tired of reading. For now, there was nothing else in the room besides that chair. But, seeing the kitchen, Urmila was truly stunned. What wasn't there? It was as if everything was for Prarthana alone. "Himu, please don't be upset that I'm asking this, but would you have done all this even if Prarthana wasn't in your life?" Urmila finally asked. Himavant, who was heating milk on the stove, turned to look at her for a moment. Seeing the silence in his eyes, Urmila felt that perhaps she shouldn't have asked such a question. But by then, Himavant had already begun to speak. "If I had never met a girl named Prarthana, you would never have seen the Himavant standing before you today, Urmila. Who tells a roaring ocean that today is a full moon? On all other days, the ocean remains within itself, so why does it fall into such a frenzy when the full moon arrives? It’s the same with me. I was just a man wandering the streets of Shivamogga, selling sweets. I never even had the thought of building a home of my own. Look at me today; how many people am I building homes for? If I fell ill, wasn't there a hospital in Shivamogga for me to lie down in? There were plenty. But I set out to build a hospital for those who fall ill—that is the power of love, Urmila; it turns a human into a god. From the moment I wake up in the morning until the very moment I drift into sleep at night, whatever I do, it only for Prarthana. If I plant a sapling, it is because, many years from now..."

## Page 202

Prarthana sits under this tree and hums in a soft voice; that is why I water it. A person in love is incredibly selfish. As Urmila's selfishness grows, love only deepens; and that is not just the selfishness of wanting to possess her. It is not sleeping with her, but rather, when eating a morsel of food mixed with a little curd, the thought of her makes it feel so delicious. Many years ago, in Birur, I felt as if Prarthana’s thumb had been split in the same way some girl had tripped, broken her big toe, and cried with a stinging pain in her eyes. And behind that, how much further is Davangere? At most, two and a half years. Prarthana will surely come. To how many thousands of such girls will she stand as a sister? Thinking of that, I swell with pride, feeling like an ocean caught in the pull of a full moon. Urmila, if she were not there, could I have done any of this? If I think of a day without her, then for me, the sun does not rise; dreams do not take birth. You know, a person who cannot conceive a dream cannot achieve anything. In Himavant’s voice, there was an honesty that only gods possess. Although Urmila wanted to ask the question, "If, by any chance, we lose Prarthana... what will you do, Himavant?" she did not have the courage. But, as if to make Urmila shudder, Himavant himself began to speak of that very matter. For some reason, his lips trembled, his eyes turned to stone. A whirlwind seemed to rise from within him, beginning to envelop him. "I love her so much, Urmila. Have I ever thought about what might happen if she becomes unreachable to me, if Prarthana slips out of my hands? For some reason, I am terrified, Urmila. Not about her—it is the fear itself that terrifies me. Because I know very well: the day she leaves my life, the foundations of every house I have built in the fields of Gadikoppa will crumble. The water in the wells I have dug will dry up. Every sapling I have planted will wither from the roots up. The milk of the cow in my backyard will turn to poison. You do not know, Urmila, but total destruction falls like rain pouring from the sky." Saying this, he stopped. For a long time, both of them did not speak. Then, taking Urmila along, he showed her, with great excitement, a small garden he had built right next to the hospital, where none of the saplings were ones he had [planted] before.

---

## Page 203

It seemed like things he had never seen before, gathered from all over his wanderings; he had brought and planted all sorts of medicinal herbs: perhaps they might come in handy for Prarthana someday. In the shed next to the garden stood a gentle cow. It had calved just fifteen days ago. A fluffy white calf with black eyes and soft lips, overflowing with love, began to jump the moment it saw Himavant. Urmila went over quickly and hugged it like a flower. She felt they could give it a beautiful name. "Its name is Prarthana," Himavant, who was standing behind her, spoke just as her thought was blooming. Urmila fell silent. The very thing she had come to Shivamogga to tell Himavant—she could not muster the courage to speak about it. Let Himavant not know about all that; at least, not right now. Why break a dream that is destined to shatter today? Nothing would turn out as terrifyingly as Himavant thought. He would be wounded, true; but just as all wounds in the world heal, the wound in his heart would also gradually heal. He would begin life again from somewhere else. No matter who goes astray, deceives, she would at least remain to fulfill the contract of friendship with a friend like Himavant. Does she have the power to stop the clouds of total destruction from opening their mouths? She did not know; Himavant would grant that too. She had to learn that from him alone. These houses in Gadikoppa, this deity on the mound, the hospital surrounding the deity, the saplings in the garden, the tree in front of the house, the calf jumping in the backyard—everything must be preserved just like this. Even after Prarthana's departure, she must ensure that Himavant’s life does not scatter. Does she have that power? Urmila asked herself again. The answer stood before her: love possesses all power. Then, is it true that she loves Himavant? She asked herself. It felt like the answer could not be found for the time being. Before leaving for Shivamogga, for some reason, Urmila touched her forehead to Himavant’s feet in reverence, but Himavant’s legs had gone numb. He did not say a word. After seeing her off and seating her on the bus to Channarayapatna, he said, "This time you didn't tell me anything about Prarthana. Don't do that next time. Come prepared to talk only about her; wait for a couple of months."

---

## Page 204

"I cannot step out of Gaadikoppa," he had said; "Has Prarthana really left?" Urmila shook her head. The bus departed. Urmila’s mind remained fixed in the innocent, pitch-black eyes of a calf named Prarthana, which was leaping in the backyard of the house. Hadn't those same eyes of Prarthana held that same innocence just two years ago? She wondered. Looking out the window, she saw rows of houses on the outskirts of Shivamogga.

Debu walked up with indifference, inserted the key into the interlock of the front door, and opened the lock with a click. Seeing the scene inside, he was stunned. The house was the exact opposite of his state of mind. Prarthana’s patience, love, devotion, and agility were reflected in every orderly corner of the house. Everything was spotless. It was only that day that he noticed there was a prayer room in the house. In the last two years, all the books he had read and discarded were neatly stacked there. There was no clutter. small photograph of Ishwara. Ishwara with half-closed eyes on the snowy mountain, the Ganga gushing from his matted hair, his skin a shade of blue, and the serpent with its hood raised—the eyes of the serpent looked alert. Come to think of it, shouldn’t Himavant be Ishwara? For just a moment, the question slithered by like a serpent's hood. It must be. Himavant must be Ishwara. That is why she had brought and placed the photo of Ishwara. For her, Himu is God, and he is exactly where he should be. But where is his own place? Was Sujay Malhotra in the place have been that night? God knows. Prarthana is not opening her mouth. Every time he sits or stands, the same confusion lingers. Was he truly there? Enough to bruise her entire body? He had met him at the college in the morning. He spoke as usual. He felt the urge to slap his face, grab his shirt, drag him like a leopard into the corner of the corridor, press him against the stone wall, and ask, "Tell me, what happened that day?" But he could not ask. What if, instead of answering, Sujay Malhotra just laughed, pulled his shirt from his grasp, straightened the wrinkles, wiped the blood from the corner of his lips, and walked away in silence? And if even he chose not to say what happened on the Harihara road, then what would be his own fate? That, above all else.

---

## Page 205

He did not want to say 'hello' to Sujay who was coming from the opposite direction, nor did he want to sit in the cinema hall just to pass the time; he had reached a point where he felt he needed nothing at all. He sat on the stone platform and smoked a cigarette. The thought of being here was becoming tiresome; his mind was unwilling to accept it, as if his very entrails were being wrung out. A junior girl came by on a Kinetic; she stopped the vehicle in the shade of a tree, adjusted the scarf that was wrapped tightly around her neck, and asked mockingly, "Why so looking melancholy? Couldn't find any girlfriends? Feeling lonely? If your Mercedes car is boring you, tell me; I’ll take you around the town on my Kinetic. Your mood will brighten up, what do you say?" Deb knew her; name was Susan Rodrigues, an Anglo-Indian girl with blue, eyes. She had been with him for ten days last year. Throughout those days, he had behaved in a way that gave him a strange sense of satisfaction—that he was deceiving this blue-eyed girl so elegantly and easily, without her ever understanding it. On the eleventh day, he had brought her back to Davanagere and said, "Enough, Susan. Enough is enough. My life is more important to me than all this. There are only two months left for the exams; I need to sit and study. You won't find me from tomorrow. Let's not meet." When he said that, she had wept as if the valves of her heart were breaking. But that was only for that day. The next day, she was with another boy. Deb thought she was just going around with someone else to vent her hatred and frustration towards him. Within two months, the two of them had left their respective hostels and started living in a separate house. "To hell with you," he thought to himself and crushed the cigarette under his boot. You girls are all the same. Today, you weep so loudly that you might as well die, and by evening, you are filled with such cruelty that you kill us with your laughter. Standing next to another man, for the mistake of ten days of love, you demand the tax of an entire lifetime. I should leave everyone and go somewhere. Prarthana, Urmila—they shouldn't be there. Not even Susan—a place where no one is around. Even Kaveri’s house is a closed chapter now; the lust of the lower belly is dead. Enough, of wandering among women, enough of being drowned in the 'stuff'. It should end. I should go somewhere, be all alone, and unburden my heavy heart to someone. Who are these people? Prarthana, Urmila, Susan, the girls from Kaveri’s house.

---

## Page 206

Who were they all to him, anyway? He, his intellect, that richness—they were all a world apart. None of them were his equals. If some Prarthana were to spend half the night on the Harihara road with some Sujay Malhotra, with her body marked and bruised—why should he be so troubled? This was not love, nor friendship, nor loyalty, nor even sorrow; it was merely a knot of suspicion. She had no loyalty whatsoever. The same woman who had bowed her forehead to Himavant’s feet and prayed to him, taught him the lessons of restraint to curb his desires and longings, was the one who had now returned with her body marked on the Harihara road! He felt she wasn't even worth his grief. Yet, the moment he felt like detaching himself from here and leaving for somewhere else, why did the memory of that Himavant from Shivamogga—leaving everyone else behind—suddenly surface in his inner mind? The very man was a comfort. Perhaps, for the first time since his father, he was able to love a man this much. If he left Davangere now, his car would find its way to Shivamogga on its own. When he first saw him, he had seemed like such a dull, insipid man. Seeing Prarthana’s forehead touch his feet, he had marveled at how such men were worthy of such devotional love. Gradually, a jealousy had grown toward Himavant. But that day, when he had chased him down in his car? He had felt as though he had achieved a victory over that man. He had labored under the illusion that he was a god, hadn't he? that he had broken that illusion and reduced him to a mere mortal. He had convinced himself powerful enough to snatch a girl like Prarthana from the embrace of such a god-like man. But today, he did not feel that way. He was right. He was truly a god. Perhaps that is why Himavant remains a devotee to his god, while his Prarthana here is gradually becoming a discordant note. In his embrace, she is melting away like a moth caught in a spider’s web. The moment to end this is not far off. And yet, Himavant lives in peace in Shivamogga; he is ruled only by faith. Loyalty sustains him, and his love elevates him. But what about him? Prarthana, whom he snatched from Himavant, is now in his own arms. She is ready for everything now, but the hearth inside his own breast has gone cold. There is no desire left for the body of the woman who returned from the Harihara road; wherever he touches her, it feels like...

---

## Page 207

It feels as if it has already been touched. Even when I touched the "deplorable" women in Kaveramma’s house from the depths of my navel, I never felt like this. I was not in love with any of them. Then, why am I feeling this way for Prarthana? Am I in love? Am I really her? If so, do I love her as much as I love Himavant? I should just ask him. What is love, Himu? What does it mean to love them? After all the betrayals and the resulting pain, if we still remain, do we become gods like you, Himu? I want to live as a human for them, and yet, become a god like you, who has endured all the deceits just as I have. Show me the way, Mitra. Even if I cannot talk about all this directly, I must go and visit Himavant, I thought. This thought struck me with such intensity that I began packing a few clothes, a towel, and a brush into a small bag. Just as I decided to leave, I came home and parked the Mercedes, only to be left stunned upon seeing the state of the house—Prarthana had cleaned everything, including Ishwara’s photograph and its surroundings, and she looked like a woman who had just finished her bath. As I wondered through what magic she had managed to do all this, I remembered that neither Prarthana nor Urmila had been at college that morning, but Sujay had been there. When nothing seemed to add up, just as I was about to light a fresh cigarette, I heard a voice clearly. I rushed to the front door, and it coincided exactly with my father, mother, and Prarthana stepping over the threshold. "What is it, you lazy sloth? Should the daughter-in-law have come to fetch us from the train? Couldn't you have at least brought the car to the railway station? How are you, wretch?" my father grumbled, placing the briefcase he was carrying onto the cane chair on the veranda and pulling Debu into a tight embrace with both arms. Saying "Amma," I slipped out of my father's embrace, hugged my mother like a bear, and kissed her cheek. The gloom, the unbearable weariness, the disgust, and the uncertainty that had been weighing on me since the morning vanished in an instant, and a sense of affection blossomed instead. Immediately after stepping off the train, Chandrakant Bandopadhyay had keenly observed the young woman in the pale pink saree waiting for them, noticing through his spectacles the innocent poverty that, unknown to her, had fashioned her into a hidden gem of refinement.

---

## Page 208

She had walked up in a manner so innocent that it seemed as if she were unaware of the significance of what she was doing, standing there waiting for them at the station. Chandrakant Babu told himself, "The girl is lovely." Prarthana, however, felt no confusion. The moment she saw the tall, slender figure in white pajama-jubba and the deep-red silk saree, with skin as fair as milk, descending from the first-class compartment, she felt, "This must be why he is so beautiful." She ran up to touch their feet in reverence and took the suitcases from their hands. "You are as beautiful as your name... Prarthana," Debu’s mother had said. It felt as if a Veena from some distant era had taken the form of a woman and was speaking to her as Debu’s mother. She walked toward the station exit, looking at Prarthana’s long braid with deep affection. "If you had looked yourselves, perhaps you would have found a better daughter-in-law than me, but I am the one who found Debu. Meeting such wonderful parents convinced me why Debu is the way he is. Even though he knows you are coming, he doesn’t know for certain that you are arriving today; I didn't tell him because I wanted it to be a surprise. He must have woken up and gone to college by now; I bunked my classes and ran here to receive you. Even if my own parents had come today, I wouldn't have been this happy. I pray to God to keep this joy with me as long as I live. My God will not disappoint me, because my name is Prarthana." As they walked toward the taxi, Prarthana said in a soft voice, "Do you sing, daughter?" Debu’s father had asked. The answer to that came at night. She had prepared the meal with great devotion; there was a cozy moonlight on the terrace of the house. Spreading a mat, the four of them sat down to eat. As Debu’s mother folded a betel leaf and handed it to her husband, Chandrakant Babu lit his cigar and began to exhale small clouds of smoke. Lying down on the mat, with his head on his mother’s lap, Debu was picking out the stalks of the betel leaves that she had spread out, eating them like a little child. Sitting in the lotus position, Prarthana sang in a voice so tender that even the moonlight seemed to grow damp, drenched in joy: "Ghar Aaja Ghir Aaye."

---

## Page 209

The joy on the faces of Debu’s parents was unmistakable. They were immersed in praising her; the taste of the meal Prarthana had prepared was still lingering on their tongues. The cleanliness of the house, her soft-spoken words, the reflection of her upbringing in every gesture—the couple had clearly reached the decision that Debu had not made a mistake in choosing this girl. He was like a child playing in the celestial path. After the meal, Prarthana came down and went into the inner room. After laying out the beds for Debu’s parents in the hall and for Debu outside, she sprinkled a little water to settle the dust, served them water to drink, and said, "I will head to the hostel now." No matter how much they insisted, she refused to stay the night. Even when Debu offered to drop her off at the hostel, she stopped him. "It has been months since the three of you met, sit and talk comfortably. I have some studying to do tonight. Dad is coming in the morning, and I will be here by breakfast time. How far is the hostel from here? There are streetlights all along the way; in front of the iron gate in five minutes. Since I already told them I would be late, the watchman will be waiting at the door." Saying this, she smiled at the elderly couple once more and left.

"How did you manage to cook all that? There wasn't even a proper vessel in the house!" Debu asked in a whisper as he walked her to the house gate.

"Debu Babu, poor girls know the art of creating a meal out of nothing. More than that, love makes everything possible. By the way, I had to tell you one thing: your mom and dad are much nicer than you!" Saying this, Prarthana walked briskly toward the hostel.

By the time Debu recovered from her remark, some time had passed. As Prarthana walked out of Debu’s house, two sharp eyes began to watch her. Like a python that has tasted blood circling a dove's nest again and again, those eyes followed Prarthana’s trail. Prarthana knocked on the latch of the iron gate and stood waiting for the old hostel watchman to come. The sharp eyes moved closer. "Prarthana, I have been waiting for you since evening. Love you."

---

## Page 210

"Prarthana..." He spoke in a voice that sounded coarse, and placed his hand on Prarthana’s back. "Dog!" Prarthana turned around suddenly, startled, her voice tinged with disgust. Just then, the watchman arrived and opened the gate. The sharp-eyed figure quickly moved into the darkness on the side of the road. That figure’s name was Sujay. Urmila, sitting at the room's window, had recognized him from that distance.

She watched Prarthana as she stepped into the room with expressionless eyes. Although Urmila was aware that Sujay had come and tried to stop and talk to her near the compound, and that Prarthana had promptly resisted him, she was surprised to see how Prarthana, who had rushed from the gate to the room in a state of agitation, suddenly relaxed the moment she stepped inside. Urmila had expected Prarthana to tell her something about Sujay saw her face, but that did not happen. "What a great day! Where had you gone since morning, Didi? Debu's parents have come. My father is coming tomorrow. I am excited," she said, leaning her back against Urmila's bed and resting her head on her lap. Prarthana's eyes were shining; there was a flood of happiness. Life was taking a turn faster than she had expected, which is why she was celebrating.

Back there, Himavant was waiting for that much-anticipated moment after five years of waiting, with the patience of a sage deep in meditation. Nothing that is formed in such a hurry and at such speed lasts for a lifetime. Life is not built overnight. It requires years of penance, patience, and faith. It has to be built brick by brick. Himavant knows this. The moment he is waiting for may not arrive even after five years, but the palace of hope he has built will not collapse all at once. Every brick of it stands on solid foundation. Himavant knows this, which is why he is waiting with such endurance. After reading the letter written by Prarthana, let alone doubting her, he had not even visited Davanagere for fear that his presence might disturb her studies.

## Page 211

Prarthana does not yet understand the extent of his sacrifice, nor does she realize how his life has blossomed like a flower overnight. She is lost in her own excitement. Thinking this, Urmila caressed Prarthana’s cheek. For some reason, Urmila felt that way; perhaps because of the situation. "There is nothing I can do about this. Everything must be left to the decision of time. How time changes our lives, isn't it, Didi? Previously, when you both were talking in the car, I would sit quietly in the back seat. Now, Debu is sitting in the front, and the steering wheel is in his hands. You are standing behind me, blessing me. Aren't you, Didi? By this time tomorrow, the engagement will be over. He is a brilliant fellow, of course. After shifting to that house, I will miss you a lot, Didi. I don't know a good person or not, but I am certainly not a bad one. I might have made mistakes without knowing. You will forgive me, won't you, Didi? You must be with me tomorrow. Don't get angry with Debu and push me away." As she spoke in an excited tone while lying on Prarthana's lap, Urmila felt like asking her, "Don't you remember Himavant even once?" But she didn't ask, because she realized she was remembering Himavant more often than Prarthana was. He probably didn't even know about this matter. In the house behind the Gundi Chowltry in Davanagere, with Prarthana, Debu, and both their parents all gathered together, they were writing and signing a tragic fate for Himavant. Unbeknownst to him, she was the only one left here to shed tears on his behalf! Swallowing the exclamation, "So sorry, Himu," Urmila fell silent. Urmila stood in amazement, watching the way Prarthana behaved the next day, the changes in her demeanor, and their engagement, which proceeded as if nothing could stop it. ## Page 212

The large *kumkum* on her forehead seemed to be competing with her smile. As soon as the hostel warden told her that someone had come to meet her, she said to herself, "It must be Himavant," and rushed out of the room. It was him, indeed. She stood under the neem tree in the premises and spoke briefly with her father. The excitement of seeing her father after such a long time, the restlessness of having taken so many decisions without his knowledge, the eagerness to inquire about her mother, her elder brother, and especially her sister-in-law—none of that surfaced. Remaining completely detached, she spoke only four words and returned to her room. She had given him some money and instructed him to buy a new shirt and come to the Gundi Chatra by ten o'clock. She had already purchased a light yellow shirt and a dark blue pant for Debu from Shivamogga, even though he wasn't there. Was her behavior throughout the day the practicality of a very clever woman, or the last-minute cruelty of a heartless girl? Urmila could not decide.

After the engagement ceremony, as she was returning to the hostel after bidding farewell to everyone, the watchman at the reception handed Prarthana a packet. Inside it was the coconut candy that Prarthana loved the most. Written in bold letters on the packet was: "I had come to Davanagere; I am returning just like that. I will not disturb you. Exams are near; study well. Your Himavant." As soon as she went to her room, Prarthana collapsed onto her bed and began to wail.

*Barkha bahar aayi, ras ki phuhar layi, ankhon mein pyar layi... sajna.* Outside, it was raining heavily without pause. Standing by the window, Lata was humming a soft song by Mangeshkar. ## Page 213

Standing there slightly bent, for some reason, she reminded him of a sitar that begged to be played. Debu, who had been lying half-reclined on the bed, his desires stirred from within, began to gaze at Prarthana. Who was this girl? Whose garden did this bloom grow in? How did she effortlessly come from nowhere and establish herself here so surely? What his father said about life was true; she wasn't headstrong, but Prarthana knew very well how to implement her decisions. She didn't put on a great act before her parents, but she was different than ever before. There was a clear humility in her speech. As much as she knew they were observing her every action and work, Prarthana continued to impress them. Only his father had melted away while watching Prarthana. He believed that such a virtuous girl was needed to rein in a son who had fallen into his own whims, without him even realizing it! Apparently, he had even told his mother this before the engagement. Whenever she was around, hadn't he called out, "Prarthana, come here!" every hour, made her sit in front of him, and insisted she sing without pause? Like a wholesale medicine dealer in Davanagere, he was known to Debu’s father in a business capacity; a family friend. When Debu came to Davanagere and joined medical college, he had been his local guardian; an elder who kept a watchful eye on Debu. After many days of observing Debu hanging out with Prarthana, Uncle Shivarajayya had written a letter to his father. ## Page 216

"I am a girl from a poor family; it is impossible for me to ever repay your debt. You stood in my father’s place and performed the *sagai* (engagement). It is only because you wrote that letter that this marriage has become firm," she said, touching Uncle Shivarajaiah’s feet in reverence.

"I wrote that letter, dear, only so that you wouldn’t have to suffer what my daughter went through. Where is the question of a debt in this?" Shivarajaiah said.

"What do you mean, 'what she went through'? What happened to your daughter...?" Prarthana asked immediately.

"The world did not let her live, my daughter," he said, his voice choking with emotion.

It felt as if a thunderbolt had struck the very depths of Prarthana’s soul. She had not even imagined that Shivarajaiah’s silence could have such a profound reason behind it. Not knowing what to say to him or how to console him, she sat in silence for a long time. She hoped Uncle Shivarajaiah might say something, but it felt as though the words within him had died long ago. She touched his feet once more, rose, and left. It began to feel as if behind Debu’s face, there was another, more terrifying face.

However, neither of Debu’s parents was ready to accept this as an official engagement. In a town that wasn't their own, with no relatives around—did they think it was over just because four people sat and talked? It might have been years since they left Kolkata, but in Hyderabad, where they had settled, they had a world of their own. They had relatives, Bengali families, and Muslim friends. If they held the engagement of their only son in secret, would people let them be? They would think Chandranath Babu was just trying to save money.

"There is no other way. Officially, according to tradition, another 'Sagaisang' must take place. Come to Bengaluru in the Mercedes, and from there, fly to Hyderabad. The travel agent will bring you the air tickets tomorrow," Debu’s father had commanded before leaving. He hadn't listened even when told that the exams were very close; he had simply said to manage and come over the weekend. He had offered to arrange air tickets for her friends as well, but when they said they couldn't come with exams approaching, he had remained silent.

On Friday evening, with two suitcases in the Mercedes, Debu drove the car toward Hiriyur, listening to songs in silence, not speaking a word.

## Page 217

As the car continued to cross the stretch, Prarthana asked in a voice that sounded quite natural, "Does Shivarajayya uncle have a daughter?" Debu was startled, but he didn't show it. "He had one," he replied briefly. "What do you mean 'had'? Doesn't she exist now?" Prarthana asked. "No; she died. She killed herself." "Committed suicide?" she asked, her voice rising and falling. "Why?" "God knows. Why does everyone commit suicide? They do it when they are bored with life; she must have done the same." Saying this, he stopped. "What was her name?" Prarthana asked persistently. "Some stupid name. Sarvamangala, I think. Let it go. It's a good thing she died. So ugly. Forget it, why bring that up now? Don't go anywhere without my knowledge; I don't like it," he said, and after that, he didn't speak a single word until they reached Bengaluru. For the first time, Debu felt that she was beginning her life with a murderer. As she stepped out of the car in front of the Windsor Manor hotel in Bengaluru, she felt like taking a bus back to Davanagere—no, to Shivamogga—and just leaving. She couldn't go. It rained very heavily in Bengaluru that night. Wearing a nightie, Prarthana stood by the wide-open window, watching the rain. That was when Debu felt she was like a sitar, and the question arose whether he should play it. But he felt as though he had become numb, unable to even rise from the bed he was lying on. This was because two names kept haunting him repeatedly: Sarvamangala and Sujay Kulhotra.

By the next morning, Prarthana had woken up with a completely different mindset at Windsor Manor.

## Page 218

The pleasant weather of Rona brought a new sense of liveliness. After finishing her bath, she kissed Debu’s forehead with her petals-of-parijata lips. They shared a cup of steaming coffee. For some reason, all the weariness and bitterness of the night had vanished, and a new longing had sprouted in both of them. After many days, Debu was dressed like a pure Bengali in a white jubba and tight pyjamas. Prarthana had emerged immersed in a pure silk saree, smelling of the earth. He kissed her so deeply and for so long that he couldn't pull away. Just as their bodies were about to tune into each other, forgetting all that was old, the first flight to Hyderabad had arrived. Leaving the Mercedes car they had brought from Davanagere at the hotel, they had rushed to the airport in a taxi. There was a sense of wonder in Prarthana’s eyes. "Truly, am I boarding a plane?" The only memory she had was of holding her brother’s hand in the courtyard of their house with the white stone pillars beyond Channarayapatna, looking up at the sky. A small, bright light, like a tiny moving star amidst the clusters of the night, was a plane—had she ever imagined this? She had only felt that perhaps some prince would seat her on a horse, ride off to an unknown direction, reach a palace chamber, and say, "Prarthana, you are mine now." Beyond that, where were the dreams? Back then, when Himavant, who had walked with her all the way from Channarayapatna to Shivamogga, was washing the wound on her broken big toe and bandaging it, she had felt she had found her black-horse prince.

Now, Debu was fastening the airplane seatbelt around her waist. In the eyes of the air hostess, there was a faint flicker—from where to where had Prarthana arrived? As the plane kicked off the ground and stood in the sky, rising higher and higher, a sunbeam piercing through the window began to play golden games on Debu’s cheeks. A man so handsome, so wealthy, so intelligent—could he not be even better? "Debu?" she said. "What?" "We will always hold each other like this, and love each other so intensely that we cannot bear to be apart even for a moment, right?" she asked. "I swear. Above all, you are my wife. love," he exclaimed, leaning toward her. The air hostess, standing at the tip of the curved airplane aisle, turned her face away with a slight impatience.
